# 1600\n
{Oxford, Bodleian Library Laud Misc 108, part 1, fols. 1r-200v, hand A: Life of Christ, Infancy of Christ, South English Legendary}\n
C13b2-C14a1\n
429 221 N\n
{=W Oxon=}\n
{=Sample tagged: fols. 1r-22r, 31v-41v: Life of Christ, Infancy of\n
Christ, SS. Saints Barnabe, John the Baptist, James the Great, Oswald and Edward=}\n
{=Script - Textura semi-quadrata=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The texts written by scribe A seem to be in very similar language\n
indeed even down to very characteristic details (e.g. zW- for OE hw-\n
GUOD for "good") and amalgamation into a single text profile seems justified\n
Such differences as there are (e.g. Life - preferred HEO "they",\n
TWEI "two", -zT- for OE -ht and FETTE "fetch" against Infancy - HUY, TWO\n
-zHT and FECHCHE) can be put down to constrained selection from\n
exemplar preferences. The figura for Z used by this scribe is of the\n
barred type and is Z-shaped not yogh-shaped. I have realised it\n
throughout as Z. I assume where it does not imply [z] that it stands\n
for [(e)t] or [(e)8] (8 is phonetic theta phonetic edh) from the\n
Latin and A-N abbreviation for <et>. Cf. WIZ is "with". Punctus\n
elevatus is not very different from colon in this hand, but there\n
usually seems to be a slight off-stroke to the upper mark of the two,\n
so I realise all examples as .\' rather than : throughout. There are\n
dots (or perhaps prickings - it is hard to tell from the microfilm)\n
marking the end of the writing space for each verse line.\n
Occasionally, when the writing comes right up to them, it is\n
impossible to tell for sure that they are not intended as punctus.\n
But even in these cases, unless there seems a particular reason to do\n
so, I do not count them as punctuation marks=}\n
{281}\n
{Oxford, Bodleian Library Laud Misc 108, part 1, hand A, fols. 1r-10v, Life of Christ}\n
{13b2-C14a1}\n
{429 221}\n
{~f1r~}\n
{=text begins imperfectly. This item, \'The Life of Christ\', and the next,\n
\'The Infancy of Christ\', are numbered items 8 and 9, suggesting that\n
items 1-7 as well as the beginning of 8 have been lost from the\n
beginning of the MS before the present fol. 1r. The end of no. 8 was\n
also probably lost at the same time, as the note in the bottom margin of\n
fol. 10v suggests that the end of the poem was probably misbound at one\n
time into the earlier part of the book. See below before f11r.\n
Punctuation is confined to mid-line punctus elevatus=}\n

that OE /y/ can be spelled <i>/<e>/<u> in ME and the <u>/<eo>\n

{=ins, in bottom margin in a slightly later hand. This note\n
suggests that the rest of the poem may at one time have been\n
misbound at the beginning of the MS. This cannot now be verified\n
as the first 7 items of the MS and the beginning of this item\n
(no. 8) are lost=}\n
{\\\\}\n
{283}\n
{Oxford, Bodleian Library Laud Misc 108, part 1, hand A, fols. 11r-22r, Infancy of Christ}\n
{C13b2-C14a1}\n
{429 221}\n
{~f11ra~}\n
{=This item \'The Infancy of Christ\', is numbered item 9 (cf. the previous\n
in the same hand, \'The Life of Christ, numbered no. 8). This suggests that\n
items 1-7 as well as the beginning of 8 have been lost from the\n
beginning of the MS before the present fol. 1r. The rhymes in this\n
verse are often very poor - half rhyme is plentiful as well as\n
assonance. In most cases it is hard to see, even if the original dialect\n
were different, how the rhymes could ever have been other than rough.\n
I have, however in almost all cases marked the words in rhyming\n
position in the usual way as the intention seems to have been to\n
write in rhyming couplets and however rough, the words chosen are\n
clearly affected by the necessity of producing something like a rhyme.\n
\'The Infancy of Christ\' is in the same hand as \'The Life of Christ\'.\n
The scribe uses a rather smaller version of his script and here writes\n
in two columns rather than across the page. There is no punctuation,\n
other than paragraph markers, in the verse. The prose section headings\n
do occasionally have some punctuation=}\n
{> para (\'*ICI CO\\MENCE LE EN\\FAUNCE IHesU Cr^IST .\'(>}\n

$see/vSpt23_SE\n
{=The syntax demands plural not singular. This scribe\'s language has\n
usual (I)SEIzE(N) for pret pl of "see" but it is hard to see how else to\n
parse this. Horstmann (1875) thinks yAT is an error for yER and\n
presumably would read SE as inf. But this scribe has SEO(N) usually\n
for the infinitive. So something seems to be not quite right with the\n
line=}\n

$little/aj-voc_LUTTLE\n
$ribaud/n-voc{rh}_*RIBAUT\n
{=Horstmann (1875) suggests for MS HEPEDE either "he paid" or\n
that it is an error for HE SEDE "he said". Assuming "paid", he\n
also punctuates the line with a colon after LUTTLE as if to\n
imply "he satisfied Jesus little: Ribaud etc". But the form in\n
which the word is written doesn\'t suggest the word "paid", while\n
"said" makes little sense. In the context of a verbal assault\n
(SAUzT) it seems most likely to be a verb meaning "cry out". For a\n
possibility, though it would have to assume an intrusive initial <h>,\n
see MED s.v. e:pen v. OED s.v. epe v. (only one citation from Orm).\n
It is just possible perhaps that HEPEDE is from the verb he:apian\n
in the sense "load onto, overwhelm". In either interpretation, the\n
syntax would have to include LUTTLE as part of the address - "little\n
worthless thing"=}\n

$country/n<pr{rh}_CONTREZ\n
{=sic. Note that this is an important early citation of the \'contreth\'\n
spelling (borne out by the rhyme on BETH) suggesting also long open\n
[e] and unvoiced final fricative. This is from eOF contrede, otherwise\n
not recorded in English until the 15th cent. See Derek Britton,\n
Neophilologus 76 (1992), 283-89. This citation may have been missed in\n
the dictionaries because of lack of understanding that in eME (and\n
regularly in this hand) the equivalent of -eth could be spelled -ez=}\n

# 282\n
{Oxford, Bodleian Library, Laud Misc 108, hand B, fols. 200v-203v: Debate between Body and Soul}\n
C13b2-C14a1\n
553 280 N\n
{=Ely (Cambs)=}\n
{=Sample represents all the text in English in this hand=}\n
{=Script - Textura semi-quadrata=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The text starts after the end of South English Legendary (in Hand A)\n
halfway through fol. 200v. The first 6 \'stanzas\' (24 long lines) are on fol.\n
200v written tightly. With Hand A\'s contribution above there are 45 lines to\n
this page. From fol. 201r the writing is more spaced with only 34 long lines to\n
the page. This gives it a look of being in a different hand, but the whole\n
text is in fact written by scribe B. Presumably he wanted to finish at the end of\n
a verso folio (which he does) and calculated back how many lines he would need\n
to get into the first half folio. The long lines rhyme aaaa but the mid-line\n
also rhymes bbbb, so if the verse were set out in short lines each \'stanza\'\n
would be abababab. The verse is not, however, organised in stanzas, there being\n
no gaps between the long lines. The punctuation is confined to punctus elevatus\n
(occasionally missing, and once a punctus) marking the mid-line rhyme. In the\n
first \'stanza\' only, the line ends are linked by a squiggly brace, presumably to show\n
the 4-line rhyming structure=}\n
{~f200v~}\n
$as/cj_[]LS\n

$destrele:de/npl<pr{rh}_DESTER-LEDD+ES $le:de/npl<pr-k{rh}_-LEDD+ES $/pln<pr_+ES\n
{=Linow (1889) emends to "that thou about in destre (i.e. right hand)\n
leads", taking "about" from the Auchinleck text which is, however,\n
somewhat different here. I assume that the Laud text here is correct\n
and the compound is referring to the trappings/bridle/leads that were\n
suitable for a quality charger or war-horse - destr(i)er. See MED s.v.\n
le:de n. 1 (or less likely) ladde n. 2) for the second part of the\n
compound. MED following Linow\'s text quotes this as the single\n
example of "destre" used in English for "right hand". But I think the\n
compound interpretation is more likely here=}\n

$sink?/vSpt23_SENK+E $/vSpt23_+E\n
{=The vocalism is troublesome here (one would expect SUNK-), but it is\n
difficult to see what else this could be read as. The sense of the whole\n
clause  would be "descended upon him on all sides". The other versions\n
closest to Laud (Auchinleck and Vernon - neither of them in LAEME) have\n
nothing helpful, and the readings are all problematic suggesting that\n
there may be some textual corruption here, especially as this half line\n
seems to bear little relation to what comes before (the torture of being forced\n
to drink hot lead) or after=}\n
$about{p}/pr_A+BOUTEN $a-/xp-pr_A+\n
{=A letter (perhaps U) erased between O and U=}\n
$/P13<prI_HIM\n
$abroad/av{rh}_A-BROD\n
{"on all sides"}\n
{=The alternative would be to read A BROD as "a brood [of foul devils]".\n
But SENKE formally can hardly be singular whether it is from the strong\n
verb sincan (where one would expect SANK with no final E) for past tense\n
or an -eth type ending if there were a switch to present tense. Equally\n
if it were from the  weak verb scencan "to plunge, drown" one would\n
expect an -eth type ending in present tense and an -ed type in the past\n
tense. But in any case this text does not have S for OE sc. For this\n
verb one would also therefore expect initial SCH- and the verb "sink"\n
seems the only possible candidate=}\n
